                            <div class="player-info-box-header team-diamondbacks"><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Alex Avila</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team"><a href="/teams/diamondbacks">Arizona Diamondbacks</a></div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 31</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: L/R</div>

            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Avila was the Tigers' main catcher for the 2010 season and he didn't disappoint... behind the plate.  He did not have a good season hitting, though.  In 2009, in just 72 PAs, he hit .279 with five home runs.  In 2010, he definitely didn't do as good, with a .228 average and seven home runs.  The home runs were nice but the batting average was brutal. This year seems like it could be a make or break one for Avila.  He should feel more comfortable catching Detroit's pitchers and can focus a little more on his offense. It wouldn't be surprising to see 15 home runs with a .275 average, if he gets plenty of plate appearances.  Also he should be plenty of chances to get plenty of R+RBI with the Tigers good offense. A huge question mark for Avila is how the Tigers plan on using recently signed Victor Martinez  The current talk is that Avila will be the everyday catcher with V-Mart giving him a day off every week or so.  If Avila gets off to a slow start like he did in 2010, V-Mart may be forced in to the catcher role more and more.   Expect Avila to get drafted in deeper and AL-only leagues.  (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Avila should improve in 2011, but, with the signing of Victor Martinez, he role isn't perfectly defined yet.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Avila always showed power and patience in the Minor Leagues. He's also a strong defensive catcher. Kudos to the Tigers for developing another skilled player at a skill position. But, like Austin Jackson before him showed, not all breakouts are created equal. Avila's .366 batting average on balls in play is not likely to be repeatable, and with his strikeout rate and batted ball profile, he's likely to have a much worse batting average in his sophomore season. That's okay. The power and patience should stick around, and the catching position as a whole puts up poor batting averages. Just don't draft Avila as a .300 hitter. (Eno Sarris)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The power, patience and defense are all real. Avila will be a good player this year. He just won't have as nice of a batting average. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Expectations were high as Avila entrenched himself into the upper echelon of catchers after his breakout 2011 campaign that included power and batting average. But the inflated batting average on balls in play he enjoyed that year couldn't be repeated, and it fell back to a much more sustainable level, taking his batting average with it. The power dropped too, but it was primarily due to a precipitous decline in fly ball rate. That should rebound some and with more at-bats and better health as well, Avila could be headed right back into the mid-teens in home run power. He'll likely be an undervalued asset on draft day and could reward fantasy owners with a nice profit. (<a target="_blank" href="http://www.pallocale.com/">Mike Podhorzer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>After a breakout 2011 season, Avila disappointed during his follow-up campaign, as he lost his batted ball magic and his power fell dramatically. While a .290 batting average won't happen again, a rebound in fly ball rate is all that is needed to get his home run total back into the mid-teens.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Do you, the intelligent reader, remember when Alex Avila was a superb offensive catcher and top-notch fantasy option? Yeah, me too, and that was all the way back in 2011, and he hasn’t been worth a darn in fantasy leagues since. Avila hit .295 with 19 homers in that glorious season, but he smacked down to a .227 average last year with only 11 dingers, and that makes me sad. Avila is somehow only in his age-27 season now, so there’s still a chance he figures some of it out again and manages to be a fringe option thanks to the team around him. Hopefully Avila is still an option this year in on-base percentage leagues, but I wouldn’t bet on anything. (Zach Sanders)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Alex Avila was a sexy fantasy catcher all the way back in 2011, but those days seem long gone. Still just in his age-27 season, there’s the hope that Avila will be a solid option in on-base percentage leagues this year.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Avila is quickly becoming a second tier catcher because of his inability to repeat his 2011 home run total of 19. After that season, his home run totals have been 9, 11, and 11 in order. The problem with the power hasn't been his home run per fly ball rate, which has always hovered around 14%-15%. The problem has been his inability to make contact (33% strikeout rate) and put the ball in the air (1.5 ground balls per fly ball). Ten home runs is probably about right for a 2015 projection.  Additionally, his average is on a four-year decline (.295 to .243 to .227 to .218) which corresponds to a four-year rise in his strikeout rate (24% to 24% to 30% to 33%).  With some regression, a .230 average with a 30% K% should be the projection here. He hasn't stolen a base in two seasons, so expect none in 2015. The biggest issue with Avila will be his health after missing days to concussions the last two seasons. Even healthy, though, a .230/10/0 catcher is not that valuable in most leagues. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>After becoming a fantasy darling a few season back, Alex Avila's inability to make contact has destroyed his fantasy value.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>A left knee issue limited Avila to 67 games in 2015, and the Tigers opted to move forward with James McCann as their catcher. Avila landed on the south side of Chicago on a $2.5 million deal, and when the team non-tendered Tyler Flowers, it seemed Avila may have another full time gig on his hands. Instead, the White Sox quickly pivoted to add Dioner Navarro, and it seems the two might be locked into a platoon -- Navarro's a switch-hitter who slugs lefties and is adequate against righties, while Avila is a lefty with typical platoon splits. A split role more or less takes Avila off the fantasy table, even if protecting him from southpaws would improve a batting average that hasn't topped .227 since 2012. Were Navarro to go down, Avila would be a candidate for 10 home runs (he hits too many ground balls to rediscover his 2011 pop), but given the 30% strikeout rate, not even a solid batted ball profile can keep him from being a weight on your average. Avila remains a solid baseball player, walking a lot and defending fairly well, but he's not a fantasy asset. (Blake Murphy)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Coming off of a disappointing season lost in part to injury, Avila finds himself in a time share with Dioner Navarro. Given Avila's inability to make contact and potentially limited playing time, he has little-to-no fantasy relevance.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Who's baseball's Three True Outcomes King the last three years? Probably Chris Davis, yeah? Or Chris Carter? Close, but not quite. It's actually Miguel Sano. But who's second? At this point, you probably know. Strikeouts, walks and home runs make up more than half his plate appearances. Unfortunately, home runs make up a significantly smaller portion of his three true outcomes than they do for the aforementioned names. His power prevents his bat from being completely incompetent — he has actually been 5% better than the league since his debut in 2009 — but his batting averages of late would make Mario Mendoza blush. His patience is admirable: only three hitters have swung at outside pitches less often than Avila since his debut. (Then again, only Carter has made less contact on such pitches than Avila during that time. At least he knows his weakness.) He's Chris Iannetta's long-lost brother, a defense-first catcher who isn't the strongest hitter but also ain't afraid of taking a free pass (or two (or eight)). A full season's worth of playing time in 2016 would have paced out to 2.5-ish WAR - impressive, yes, had he not hit so poorly that he lost his job to James McCann. After a year in Chicago, he returns to Detroit, presumably to assume his same backup role behind McCann. Even playing full-time, any employment of Avila would be defensible only in OBP-only leagues. Until then, he's not fantasy relevant. (Alex Chamberlain)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Re-signed by the Tigers, Avila figures to relieve James McCann and doesn't figure to produce much value unless his batterymate gets injured. Even then, Avila's extreme strikeout problems make him a justifiable option only in AL-only OBP-based formats.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>

Martinez was the only batter with more than 100 plate appearances who made more hard contact. That offensive renaissance earned Avila more playing time on both the Tigers and Cubs, and he turned his 376 plate appearances into 14 home runs. Given his lack of speed, you have to figure his .382 batting average on balls in play is going to decline, but it can’t fall too far if Avila continues to crush the ball. Meanwhile, a regular catcher that can hit .250 and 15 home runs is a useful fantasy player, and when he also walks in 17% of his plate appearances and plays solid defense, he’s a borderline All-Star. His recent performance but lack of track record will make it interesting to see where Avila lands in free agency and in what role.                 <div id="SeasonStats1_tsStats" class="RadTabStrip RadTabStrip_FanGraphs RadTabStripTop_FanGraphs RadTabStripTop">

            <td align="right" style="width: 805px">
